{"uri": "2024-07-428031709", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "11:48:10", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T11:48:10Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T03:53:28Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9529411792755127, "url": "https://www.aol.com/missouri-woman-served-43-years-035328312.html", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her murder conviction was overturned", "body": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her murder conviction was overturned\n\nAssociated Press\n\nJuly 20, 2024 at 5:53 AM\n\nChillicothe, Missouri (AP) - A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years of a life sentence was released Friday, despite attempts in the last month by Missouri's attorney general to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 64, left a prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nHemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after he ordered her to be freed in her own recognizance. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nHemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release.\n\nO'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The next day, July 9, Horsman ruled Hemme should be released to go home with her sister. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nFor more CNN news and newsletters create an A ruling by the Missouri Supreme Court has paved the way for Sandra Hemme, a woman whose murder conviction was overturned, to be released from prison after serving 43 years. Hemme has been in custody at the Chillicothe Correctional Center.\n\nCHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 63, left prison Friday in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her daughter and granddaughter. Her sister, Joyce Ann Kays, was all grins.\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay was causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, Missouri.", She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nHemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after he ordered her to be freed in her own recognizance. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nHemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release.\n\nO'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The next day, July 9, Horsman ruled Hemme should be released to go home with her sister. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "independent.com.mt", {"uri": "2024-07-427653443", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-19", "time": "23:59:52", "dateTime": "2024-07-19T23:59:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-19T19:22:25Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9411764740943909, "url": "https://wgxa.tv/news/nation-world/woman-missouri-sandra-hemme-served-43-years-prison-release-free-1980-murder-conviction-tossed-overturned", "title": "Woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her 1980 murder conviction was tossed", "body": "CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nOn Friday, Sandra Hemme left prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release.\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts. Attorneys for Hemme, 64, say they are thrilled.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay is causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still behind bars late Friday, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman was opposition from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press on Thursday, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already been in prison for decades for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could (HG Biggs/The Kansas City Star via Associated Press)\n\nCHILLICOTHE, Missouri -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, July 19, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 63, left prison Friday in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office for contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter, and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nREAD: US man sentenced to death free 2 years after murder conviction reversed\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office for contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said the US woman could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\n\"She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. Her father was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nHe said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court, and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force the US woman to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, whose health is ailing.\n\nSean O'Brien, Hemme's attorney, said he's thrilled she will be released.\n\n\"This has been a long time coming. We are headed to the prison now to walk her out. She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. \"It's not over. There's a lot more to do but this is a big step.\"\n\nA circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court have all agreed that a woman whose murder conviction was overturned should be free after 43 years in prison.\n\nYet Hemme is still behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman is opposition from Missouri Attorney General Andrew Bailey, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor {"uri": "2024-07-427676673", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "01:06:35", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T01:06:35Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T00:51:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9372549057006836, "url": "https://www.register-herald.com/news/nation_world/missouri-woman-who-served-43-years-in-prison-is-free-after-her-murder-conviction-was/article_a9676d2d-3c9f-5636-9fc3-c22400604b23.html", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her murder conviction was overturned", "body": "CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years of a life sentence was released Friday, despite attempts in the last month by Missouri's attorney general to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 64, left a prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nHemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after he ordered her to be freed in her own recognizance. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nHemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release.\n\nO'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, Missouri.", Her sister, Joyce Ann Kays, was all grins.\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nAlso read | Bengal dancer shot dead in US, kin desperately seek govt help to bring body\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 pm CDT Friday\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care.\n\n\"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay was causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\" After she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing.\n\n\"This has been a long time coming.\" Over the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case.\n\nThe Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nAlso read | Inequality, polarising politics and neoliberal policies: Lessons for India from America's decline\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the US, according to her legal team at the Innocence Project\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\" Some legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing.\n\nHer She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nHemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after he ordered her to be freed in her own recognizance. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nHemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release.\n\nO'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The next day, July 9, Horsman ruled Hemme should be released to go home with her sister. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried {"uri": "2024-07-427642519", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-19", "time": "23:25:52", "dateTime": "2024-07-19T23:25:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-19T22:59:30Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9333333373069763, "url": "https://www.kulr8.com/news/national/missouri-woman-who-served-43-years-in-prison-is-free-after-her-1980-murder-conviction/article_2ee5e60a-ddf2-59d3-b7af-7a801287546c.html", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her 1980 murder conviction was overturned", "body": "CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nOn Friday, Sandra Hemme left prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release.\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence.\" But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts. Hemme is now 64 years old. Her attorney says they are thrilled.\n\nTHIS IS A BREAKING NEWS UPDATE. AP's earlier story follows below.\n\nCHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A Missouri judge has once again ruled that a woman whose 1980 murder conviction was overturned must be released -- and he said she must be allowed to walk free by Friday evening.\n\nJudge Ryan Horsman overturned Sandra Hemme's conviction last month and ordered her release or a retrial, but Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought it in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay is causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still behind bars late Friday, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman was opposition from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press on Thursday, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already been in prison for decades for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, Missouri.\n\nMore from this section\n\nComedian Bob Newhart, deadpan master of sitcoms and telephone monologues, dies at 94\n\n[NEW] 2024 Best Pok\u00e9mon GO Spoofer for iPhone and Android | Tenorshare iAnyGo\n\nSen. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, whose health is ailing.\n\nSean O'Brien, Hemme's attorney, said he's thrilled she will be released.\n\n\"This has been a long time coming. We are headed to the prison now to walk her out. She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. \"It's not over. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried Missouri Department of Corrections via AP, file\n\nSandra Hemme, 63, left prison Friday in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if it continued to fight against her release. Hemme reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her daughter and granddaughter. Her sister, Joyce Ann Kays, was all grins.\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released.\n\n\"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, who had been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. local time Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay was causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from Bailey, the attorney general, who filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center had declined to let Hemme go based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on Sunday that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court had ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. And the Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman - an officer who died in 2015 - and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, {"uri": "8234319799", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "01:00:58", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T01:00:58Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T01:00:16Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9215686321258545, "url": "https://medicinehatnews.com/news/world-news/2024/07/19/missouri-woman-who-served-43-years-in-prison-is-free-after-her-murder-conviction-was-overturned/", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her murder conviction was overturned - Medicine Hat News", "body": "SUBSCRIBE NOW CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) - A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years of a life sentence was released Friday, despite attempts in the last month by Missouri's attorney general to keep her behind bars. Sandra Hemme, 64, left a prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter. \"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\"\u009d she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\u009d Her granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\u009d Hemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\"\u009d of \"actual innocence\"\u009d and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts. \"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\"\u009d her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\u009d During a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt. He also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\"\u009d Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\u009d Hemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"\u009dThis has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release. O'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\u009d There are still struggles ahead. \"She's going to need help,\"\u009d he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long. Over the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled. \"I've never seen it,\"\u009d said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\u009d The lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions. Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\"\u009d A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law. Bailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. Hemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri. Hemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\"\u009d Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now. Her attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\u009d Some legal experts agreed. Peter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\"\u009d since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime. Bailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday. Bailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence. Horsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\"\u009d when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\"\u009d Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said. The St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman - a fellow officer, who died in 2015 - and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found. Evidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\n\"She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. Her father was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nHe said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "chicagotribune.com",
