She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Atty. Gen. Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be freed.\n\n\"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said. \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed that Hemme would be released Friday before 6 p.m. Central Time.\n\n\"She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. Her father was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nHe said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for Social Security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center had declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center in the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Mo.\n\nShe had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\nHemme's freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the 1980 slaying.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nThe attorney general, who was appointed after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- an officer who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "latimes.com", "dataType": {"uri": "8234219062", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-19", "time": "22:33:24", "dateTime": "2024-07-19T22:33:24Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-19T22:32:46Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9058823585510254, "url": "https://www.news-gazette.com/news/nation-world/missouri-judge-scolds-ags-office-and-orders-release-of-woman-whose-murder-conviction-was-overturned/article_87d20e91-fcb0-5549-9e29-7b2f572a329d.html", "title": "Missouri judge scolds AG's office and orders release of woman whose murder conviction was overturned", "body": "CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A Missouri judge has once again ruled that a woman whose 1980 murder conviction was overturned must be released -- and he said she must be allowed to walk free by Friday evening.\n\nJudge Ryan Horsman overturned Sandra Hemme's conviction last month and ordered her release then, but Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought it in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning.\n\nHe also scolded the attorney general's office for calling the warden and telling them not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" the family said in a statement.\n\nThey continued, saying further delay is causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\n\"This has been a long time coming,\" Sean O'Brien, Hemme's attorney, said. \"She is going right to her father.\"\n\nEven though a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court have all agreed on her release, she was still behind bars late Friday, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman is opposition from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already been in prison for decades for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "news-gazette.com", She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter. \"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"Her granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"Hemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\" During a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.He also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"Hemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release. O'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"There are still struggles ahead.\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long. Over the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"The lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.Bailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. Hemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.Hemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.Her attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"Some legal experts agreed.Peter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.Bailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.Bailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.Horsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.The St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.Evidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.Horsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nA woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years of a life sentence was released Friday, despite attempts in the last month by Missouri's attorney general to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 64, left a prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nCircuit Judge Ryan Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "yahoo.com", "dataType": (AP) -- A Missouri judge has once again ruled that a woman whose 1980 murder conviction was overturned must be released -- and he said she must be allowed to walk free by Friday evening.\n\nJudge Ryan Horsman overturned Sandra Hemme's conviction last month and ordered her release or a retrial, but Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought it in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay is causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still behind bars late Friday, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman was opposition from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press on Thursday, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could {"uri": "8234246310", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-19", "time": "23:11:17", "dateTime": "2024-07-19T23:11:17Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-19T23:10:08Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8745098114013672, "url": "https://www.thestar.com/news/world/united-states/missouri-woman-who-served-43-years-in-prison-is-free-after-her-1980-murder-conviction/article_cedb87c1-57e5-544d-9d00-b3218a5d590c.html", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her 1980 murder conviction was overturned", "body": "CHILLICOTHE, Mo. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nOn Friday, Sandra Hemme left prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release.\n\n\u00d7\n\nAlready a Subscriber? Sign in You are logged in Switch accounts\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts. Attorneys for Hemme, 64, say they are thrilled.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and moved to palliative care. \"He wants only to see his daughter free in his lifetime, just as Ms. Hemme wants nothing more than to be at her father's bedside at this time,\" Hemme's attorney, Sean O'Brien, said in a court filing Thursday.\n\nHe added that further delay is causing their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nAfter she is released, \"she is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said after Friday's court hearing. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still behind bars late Friday, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman was opposition from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press on Thursday, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW ARTICLE CONTINUES BELOW\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already been in prison for decades for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\n__\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nHeather Hollingsworth And Jim Salter, The Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than this confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared her, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\n\"This Court finds that the evidence shows that Ms. Hemme's statements to police are so unreliable and that the evidence pointing to Michael Holman as the perpetrator of the crime so objective and probative that no reasonable juror would find Ms. Hemme guilty,\" Horsman concluded in his 118-page ruling. \"She is the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nCopy the Story Link\n\nComments are not available on this story.\n\nSend questions/comments to the editors.\n\n\" Previous\n\nPelosi has told House Sandra Hemme has already served 43 years in prison. Judge Ryan Horsman overturned her conviction last month and ordered her release then, but Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought it in the courts. On Friday, Horsman said Hemme must be released by 6 p.m. CDT or he would bring Bailey into court next week and hold him in contempt. Hemme is now 64 years old.\n\nTHIS IS A BREAKING NEWS UPDATE. AP's earlier story follows below.\n\nA circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court agree that a woman whose murder conviction was overturned should be free after 43 years in prison.\n\nYet Sandra Hemme is still behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman is opposition from Missouri Attorney General Andrew Bailey, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nCircuit Judge Ryan Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "brandonsun.com", "dataType": One of her attorneys, Sean O'Brien, filed a motion Thursday asking that a judge \"hold an emergency status conference at the earliest possible time\" and order Hemme's release.\n\nHemme's lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme, now 64, had been serving a life sentence at a prison northeast of Kansas City after she was twice convicted of murder in the death of library worker Patricia Jeschke.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\n\"This Court finds that the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence,\" Circuit Court Judge Ryan Horsman concluded after an extensive review.\n\nHorsman noted that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than this confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\n\"Police exploited her mental illness and coerced her into making false statements while she was sedated and being treated with antipsychotic medication,\" the Innocence Project said. \"The only evidence that ever connected Ms. Hemme to the crime was her own unreliable and false confessions: statements taken from her while she was being treated at the state psychiatric hospital and forcibly given medication literally designed to overpower her will.\"\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman - a fellow officer, who died in 2015 - and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared her, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\n\"This Court finds that the evidence shows that Ms. Hemme's statements to police are so unreliable and that the evidence pointing to Michael Holman as the perpetrator of the crime so objective and probative that no reasonable juror would find Ms. Hemme guilty,\" Horsman concluded in his 118-page ruling. \"She is the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "cbsnews.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "CBS News"}, "authors": [], {"uri": "2024-07-427679434", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-20", "time": "01:14:41", "dateTime": "2024-07-20T01:14:41Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-20T01:01:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8549019694328308, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/news/local/crime-courts/missouri-woman-who-served-43-years-in-prison-is-free-after-her-1980-murder-conviction/article_6d0914f6-4633-11ef-8524-e32a83917e55.html", "title": "Missouri woman who served 43 years in prison is free after her 1980 murder conviction was overturned (copy)", "body": "The Chillicothe Correctional Center in Chillicothe, Mo., is seen on Thursday, July 18, 2024. A ruling by the Missouri Supreme Court has paved the way for Sandra Hemme, a woman whose murder conviction was overturned, to be released from prison after serving 43 years. Hemme has been in custody at the Chillicothe Correctional Center.\n\nBy HEATHER HOLLINGSWORTH and JIM SALTER Associated Press\n\nCHILLICOTHE, Mo. -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years in prison was released Friday, after Missouri's attorney general fought for more than a month to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 63, left prison Friday in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nFormer St. Louis officials Lewis Reed, John Collins-Muhammad released from federal prison Cardinals closer Ryan Helsley elects not to pitch in All-Star Game, replaced on NL roster New St. Louis Public Schools communications chief also lives in Texas St. Charles teacher sexually abused teen girl for years, police say 'Steal of the draft': Cardinals pounce at No. 7 on collegiate infielder JJ Wetherholt Cardinals TV analyst Jim Edmonds draws controversy with Cubs celebration gripe St. Louis Public Schools halts hiring over questions on new administrator from Texas Trump injured but 'fine' after attempted assassination at rally; shooter and one attendee are dead St. Louis hosts emergency and military training for massive earthquake Grades for Cardinals position players: the good, bad and ugly O'Fallon, Mo., firm to expand, add jobs with millions in federal dollars for semiconductor push Cardinals' TV ratings on record-low pace amid continuing distribution problems: Media Views MSD's recently retired CEO was paid more than $100,000 for unused vacation, sick time New report says MetroLink expansion in St. Louis would aid economy HOA rules in the St. Louis area can turn into fighting words\n\nThe judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and overturned the conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning, and he threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department then confirmed Hemme, whose been in prison for 43 years, would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\n\"She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. Her father was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming.\"\n\nHe said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, Missouri.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. (AP) -- A woman whose murder conviction was overturned after she served 43 years of a life sentence was released Friday, despite attempts in the last month by Missouri's attorney general to keep her behind bars.\n\nSandra Hemme, 64, left a prison in Chillicothe, hours after a judge threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt if they continued to fight against her release. She reunited with her family at a nearby park, where she hugged her sister, daughter and granddaughter.\n\n\"You were just a baby when your mom sent me a picture of you,\" she said. \"You looked just like your mamma when you were little and you still look like her.\"\n\nHer granddaughter laughed. \"I get that a lot.\"\n\nFormer St. Louis officials Lewis Reed, John Collins-Muhammad released from federal prison Cardinals closer Ryan Helsley elects not to pitch in All-Star Game, replaced on NL roster New St. Louis Public Schools communications chief also lives in Texas St. Charles teacher sexually abused teen girl for years, police say 'Steal of the draft': Cardinals pounce at No. 7 on collegiate infielder JJ Wetherholt Cardinals TV analyst Jim Edmonds draws controversy with Cubs celebration gripe St. Louis Public Schools halts hiring over questions on new administrator from Texas Trump injured but 'fine' after attempted assassination at rally; shooter and one attendee are dead Cardinals' TV ratings on record-low pace amid continuing distribution problems: Media Views St. Louis hosts emergency and military training for massive earthquake Grades for Cardinals position players: the good, bad and ugly O'Fallon, Mo., firm to expand, add jobs with millions in federal dollars for semiconductor push MSD's recently retired CEO was paid more than $100,000 for unused vacation, sick time New report says MetroLink expansion in St. Louis would aid economy HOA rules in the St. Louis area can turn into fighting words\n\nHemme had been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. The judge originally ruled on June 14 that Hemme's attorneys had established \"clear and convincing evidence\" of \"actual innocence\" and he overturned her conviction. But Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought her release in the courts.\n\n\"It was too easy to convict an innocent person and way harder than it should have been to get her out, even to the point of court orders being ignored,\" her attorney Sean O'Brien said. \"It shouldn't be this hard to free an innocent person.\"\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Judge Ryan Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released within hours, Bailey himself would have to appear in court Tuesday morning. He threatened to hold the attorney general's office in contempt.\n\nHe also scolded Bailey's office for calling the warden and telling prison officials not to release Hemme after he ordered her to be freed in her own recognizance. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nHemme declined to address reporters after she was released. O'Brien said she was going straight to the side of her father, who was hospitalized with kidney failure and recently moved to palliative care. \"This has been a long time coming,\" he said of her release.\n\nO'Brien said previously that delays had caused their family \"irreparable harm and emotional distress.\"\n\nThere are still struggles ahead.\n\n\"She's going to need help,\" he said, noting she won't be eligible for social security because she has been incarcerated for so long.\n\nOver the last month, a circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court all agreed Hemme should be released, but she was still held behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom came from the attorney general, who filed court motions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center initially declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 that Hemme should be set free while it continued to review the case. The next day, July 9, Horsman ruled Hemme should be released to go home with her sister. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another request late Thursday, asking the Circuit Court to reconsider.\n\nHemme was serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom was complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey had argued that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and that she should start serving those sentences now.\n\nHer attorneys countered that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agreed.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison was \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" since evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit the crime.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment Friday.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nSalter reported from O'Fallon, Missouri.\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she\u00b4s already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn\u00b4t commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey\u00b4s office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner\u00b4s effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey\u00b4s office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn\u00b4t commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell\u00b4s effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 - just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person\u00b4s DNA on the knife, but not Williams\u00b4.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman - a fellow officer, who died in 2015 - and the prosecution wasn\u00b4t told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman\u00b4s pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke\u00b4s apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nChillicothe Correctional Center, where Sandra Hemme is being held, is pictured Thursday, July 18, 2024, in Chillicothe, Mo. {"uri": "2024-07-427432430", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-19", "time": "17:25:19", "dateTime": "2024-07-19T17:25:19Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-19T16:58:06Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8352941274642944, "url": "https://www.wboc.com/news/national/three-courts-agree-that-a-woman-deemed-wrongfully-convicted-should-be-freed-she-still-isnt/article_4f50b8ac-b4a0-5ec7-a815-895abc09a616.html", "title": "Three courts agree that a woman deemed wrongfully convicted should be freed. She still isn't.", "body": "The Chillicothe Correctional Center in Chillicothe, Mo., is seen on Thursday, July 18, 2024. A ruling by the Missouri Supreme Court has paved the way for Sandra Hemme, a woman whose murder conviction was overturned, to be released from prison after serving 43 years. Hemme has been in custody at the Chillicothe Correctional Center.\n\nA circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court agree that a woman whose murder conviction was overturned should be free after 43 years in prison.\n\nYet Sandra Hemme is still behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman is opposition from Missouri Attorney General Andrew Bailey, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nCircuit Judge Ryan Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"", "source": {"uri": "wboc.com", "dataType": "title": "Missouri judge scolds AG's office and orders release of woman whose murder conviction was overturned", "body": "By HEATHER HOLLINGSWORTH and JIM SALTER - Associated Press\n\nA Missouri judge has once again ruled that a woman whose 1980 murder conviction was overturned must be released -- and he said she must be allowed to walk free by Friday evening.\n\nJudge Ryan Horsman overturned Sandra Hemme's conviction last month and ordered her release then, but Republican Attorney General Andrew Bailey fought it in the courts.\n\nDuring a court hearing Friday, Horsman said that if Hemme wasn't released by a designated time, he wanted Bailey himself to appear in court Tuesday morning.\n\nHe also scolded the attorney general's office for calling the warden and telling them not to release Hemme after an appeals court panel said she could be released. \"I would suggest you never do that,\" Horsman said, adding: \"To call someone and tell them to disregard a court order is wrong.\"\n\nThe Missouri Corrections Department confirmed Hemme would be released before 6 p.m. CDT Friday.\n\nFormer St. Louis officials Lewis Reed, John Collins-Muhammad released from federal prison area can turn into fighting words Lush gardens surround renovated historic Kirkwood home\n\nTwo of Hemme's relatives were in court Friday but declined to talk after the hearing. The rest of her family members were with Hemme's father, whose health is ailing.\n\nSean O'Brien, Hemme's attorney, said he's thrilled she will be released.\n\n\"This has been a long time coming. We are headed to the prison now to walk her out. She is going right to her father,\" O'Brien said. \"It's not over. There's a lot more to do but this is a big step.\"\n\nA circuit judge, an appellate court and the Missouri Supreme Court have all agreed that a woman whose murder conviction was overturned should be free after 43 years in prison.\n\nYet Hemme is still behind bars, leaving her lawyers and legal experts puzzled.\n\n\"I've never seen it,\" said Michael Wolff, a former Missouri Supreme Court judge and professor and dean emeritus of Saint Louis University Law School. \"Once the courts have spoken, the courts should be obeyed.\"\n\nThe lone holdup to freedom for the 64-year-old woman is opposition from Missouri Attorney General Andrew Bailey, who has filed court actions seeking to force her to serve additional years for decades-old prison assault cases. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nHorsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. The Missouri Supreme Court on Thursday declined to undo the lower court rulings that allowed her to be released on her own recognizance and placed with her sister and brother-in-law.\n\nBailey, a Republican facing opposition in the Aug. 6 primary election, responded with another appeal filed late Thursday asking the Circuit Court to reconsider. It's unclear if it will. Meanwhile, a judge in Chillicothe has scheduled an afternoon hearing in the case.\n\nHemme has been serving a life sentence at the Chillicothe Correctional Center for the 1980 stabbing death of library worker Patricia Jeschke in St. Joseph, Missouri.\n\nShe's been the longest-held wrongly incarcerated woman known in the U.S., according to her legal team at the Innocence Project. Her lawyers, in an emailed statement to The Associated Press, said her family \"is eager and ready to reunite with her, and the Department of Corrections should respect and promptly\" release her.\n\nHemme's immediate freedom has been complicated by sentences she received for crimes committed while behind bars. She received a 10-year sentence in 1996 for attacking a prison worker with a razor blade, and a two-year sentence in 1984 for \"offering to commit violence.\" Bailey argues that Hemme represents a safety risk to herself and others and the additional sentences should now be served.\n\nHer attorneys counter that keeping her incarcerated any longer would be a \"draconian outcome.\"\n\nSome legal experts agree.\n\nPeter Joy, a law professor at the Washington University School of Law in St. Louis, said the effort to keep Hemme in prison is \"a shock to the conscience of any decent human being,\" noting that she's already served 43 years for a crime that evidence strongly suggests she didn't commit.\n\n\"To now say she has to serve an additional 12 years is like running over a person and backing your car up to run over them a second time,\" Joy said.\n\nBailey's office did not immediately respond to messages seeking comment.\n\nBailey, who was appointed attorney general after Eric Schmitt was elected to the U.S. Senate in 2022, has a history of opposing overturning convictions, even when local prosecutors cite evidence of actual innocence.\n\nIn 2023, Bailey's office argued against then-St. Louis Circuit Attorney Kim Gardner's effort to overturn the murder conviction of Lamar Johnson, who was imprisoned 28 years. A St. Louis judge sided with Johnson, who was freed.\n\nBailey's office also argued in court in May against freeing Christopher Dunn, who has spent 33 years in prison for a 1990 killing that St. Louis Circuit Attorney Gabe Gore determined that Dunn probably didn't commit. A judge is still deciding that case.\n\nAnd Bailey is opposing St. Louis County Prosecuting Attorney Wesley Bell's effort to set aside the murder conviction of Marcellus Williams. A hearing is Aug. 21 -- just a month before Williams is scheduled to be executed. Testing unavailable at the time of the 1998 stabbing death found another person's DNA on the knife, but not Williams'.\n\nHorsman, after an extensive review, concluded in June that Hemme was heavily sedated and in a \"malleable mental state\" when investigators repeatedly questioned her in a psychiatric hospital after the killing. Her attorneys described her ultimate confession as \"often monosyllabic responses to leading questions.\" Other than the confession, no evidence linked her to the crime, her trial prosecutor said.\n\nThe St. Joseph Police Department, meanwhile, ignored evidence pointing to Michael Holman -- a fellow officer, who died in 2015 -- and the prosecution wasn't told about FBI results that could have cleared Hemme, so it was never disclosed before her trials, the judge found.\n\nEvidence presented to Horsman showed that Holman's pickup truck was seen outside Jeschke's apartment, that he tried to use her credit card, and that her earrings were found in his home.\n\nHorsman, in his report, called Hemme \"the victim of a manifest injustice.\"\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. The warden at the Chillicothe Correctional Center has declined to let Hemme go, based on Bailey's actions.\n\nCircuit Judge Ryan Horsman ruled on June 14 that \"the totality of the evidence supports a finding of actual innocence.\" A state appeals court ruled on July 8 ruled that Hemme should be set free. It's unclear if it will.
